08:58:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","title":" Authenticate with Firebase using Okta","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/uyRf5VaFMBI/authenticate-with-firebase-using-okta.html","description":"<meta name=\"twitter:image\" content=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-WesK75xkREw/XymKgQu8JqI/AAAAAAAAE6c/-S_lFGNoHZY8ND2KjyzK0lzm8EO5qcEHQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase-okta.png\"><img style=\"display:none\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-WesK75xkREw/XymKgQu8JqI/AAAAAAAAE6c/-S_lFGNoHZY8ND2KjyzK0lzm8EO5qcEHQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase-okta.png\"> <figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img style=\"margin:0;\" border=\"0\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-jcsEetKzGWE/Xyif8lmuiUI/AAAAAAAAE6Q/0Sn8GoHh_AU_PxBl3gmRMLIp4uDsKaIAACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Security%2BPhoto.jpg\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div>Kevin Cheung  </div></strong>      <em>Technical Writer</em>  </figcaption></figure> <p>Most apps that you build with Firebase’s backend services, such as Realtime Database, Cloud Firestore, and Cloud Storage, need some way to sign users in: among other things, this lets you provide a consistent experience across sessions and devices, and lets you set user-specific permissions. Firebase Authentication helps you meet this requirement by providing libraries and services that you can use to quickly build a new sign-in system for your app.  </p><p>But what if your organization already uses a service such as Okta to handle user identity? With Firebase Custom Authentication, you can use any user identity service (including Okta) to authenticate with Firebase, and this post will show you how. </p><p>You’ll learn how to build a Firebase and Okta integration, which will have two components: </p><ul> <li>A Node.js backend that “exchanges” Okta access tokens for Firebase custom authentication tokens. The backend is an Express.js app that you can deploy as a Cloud Function or run on your own infrastructure.  <li>A web frontend that signs users in with Okta, gets a Firebase custom authentication token from your backend, and authenticates with Firebase using the custom token. </li></ul><p>By the way, this approach can also be used with some modification for other identity services, such as Auth0, Azure Active Directory, or your own custom system. </p><p>Ready to get started? Great! But, before you write any code, you’ll need to set up your Okta and Firebase projects. </p><h2>I hope you like consoles</h2><p>First, set up an Okta project on the <a href=\"https://developer.okta.com/\">Okta Developer</a> site: </p><ol><li>Sign in or sign up for a new account. <li>Take note of your <strong>Org URL</strong> (top-right of the dashboard) for later. <li>On the <strong>Applications</strong> page, add a Single-Page App. <p>    Set the <strong>Base URIs</strong> and <strong>Login redirect URIs</strong> to the location where you plan to host your web frontend (<code>http://localhost:5000</code> if you’re using the Firebase Hosting emulator) and enable the <strong>Authorization Code</strong> grant type. </p><p>    When you’re done, take note of the app's <strong>Client ID</strong> for later. </p><li>In <strong>API > Trusted Origins</strong>, confirm that the base URI you set above is listed, with <strong>CORS</strong> and <strong>Redirect</strong> enabled. </li></ol><p>Then, set up a Firebase project in the <a href=\"https://console.firebase.google.com/\">Firebase console</a>: </p><ol> <li>Open your Firebase project or create a new one. Take note of your <strong>project ID</strong> for later.  <li>On the Project Overview page, add a new web app. <p>     If you plan to eventually host your web app with Firebase, you can automatically set up Firebase Hosting and simplify configuration by enabling <strong>Also set up Firebase Hosting for this app</strong>. </p><li>If you plan to test your token exchange endpoint locally, such as by using the Cloud Functions emulator (recommended), open your project settings and, on the Service Accounts page, generate and download an Admin SDK service account key. Be sure to keep this file safe, as it grants administrator access to your project. </li></ol><p>Finally, if you plan to deploy your token exchange endpoint as a Cloud Function: </p><ol><li><a href=\"https://console.cloud.google.com/apis/api/iamcredentials.googleapis.com/overview?project=_\">Enable the IAM Service Account Credentials API</a> in the Google Cloud console. <li>After you deploy your Cloud Function, you will also need to make sure it is configured to run as a service account with the Service Account Token Creator role. See the <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/functions-samples/tree/master/okta-auth#deploy-the-demo-to-firebase-hosting-and-cloud-functions\">sample app documentation</a> for details. </li></ol><p>Now that your projects are set up, you’ll write the crucial piece: the token exchange endpoint. </p><h2>Okta tokens in; Firebase tokens out</h2><p>The job of the token exchange endpoint is to take a user’s Okta access token and, if it’s valid, produce a Firebase custom authentication token that represents the same user. </p><p>This endpoint needs to be able to verify the authenticity of the Okta access token. To accomplish this, use the <a href=\"https://developer.okta.com/docs/guides/protect-your-api/nodeexpress/require-authentication/\">Express.js middleware</a> provided in Okta’s developer documentation (reproduced below, with minor modifications): </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">const OKTA_ORG_URL = // Your Okta org URL<br />const OktaJwtVerifier = require('@okta/jwt-verifier');<br />const oktaJwtVerifier = new OktaJwtVerifier({<br />    issuer: `${OKTA_ORG_URL}/oauth2/default`<br />});<br /><br />// Middleware to authenticate requests with an Okta access token.<br />const oktaAuth = async (req, res, next) => {<br />    const authHeader = req.headers.authorization || '';<br />    const match = authHeader.match(/Bearer (.+)/);<br /><br /><br />    if (!match) {<br />        res.status(401);<br />        return next('Unauthorized');<br />    }<br /><br /><br />    const accessToken = match[1];<br />    try {<br />        const jwt = await oktaJwtVerifier.verifyAccessToken(<br />                accessToken, 'api://default');<br />        req.jwt = jwt;<br />        return next();  // Pass the request on to the main route.<br />    } catch (err) {<br />        console.log(err.message);<br />        res.status(401);<br />        return next('Unauthorized');<br />    }<br />}<br /></pre>  <p>Any endpoint protected by this middleware will require a valid Okta access token in the <code>Authorization</code> header. If the token is valid, it will insert the decoded token into the request before passing the request along by calling <code>next()</code>. </p><p>Now, you can write the token exchange endpoint: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">const express = require('express');<br />const app = express();<br />const cors = require('cors')({origin: 'https://YOUR_DOMAIN'});<br /><br />const firebaseAdmin = require('firebase-admin');<br />const firebaseApp = firebaseAdmin.initializeApp();<br /><br />// Get a Firebase custom auth token for the authenticated Okta user.<br />// This endpoint uses the `oktaAuth` middleware defined above to<br />// ensure requests have a valid Okta access token.<br />app.get('/firebaseCustomToken', [cors, oktaAuth], async (req, res) => {<br />    const oktaUid = req.jwt.claims.uid;<br />    try {<br />        const firebaseToken =<br />                await firebaseApp.auth().createCustomToken(oktaUid);<br />        res.send(firebaseToken);<br />    } catch (err) {<br />        console.log(err.message);<br />        res.status(500).send('Error minting token.');<br />    }<br />});<br /></pre>  <p>This endpoint uses the Firebase Admin SDK to mint a Firebase custom authentication token using the user’s Okta UID. When you sign a user in with this token for the first time (on the frontend), Firebase Authentication will add a user record with the same UID to your project. </p><p>This process of using an Okta access token to acquire a Firebase custom token is the key idea behind integrating Okta and Firebase. But, let’s go one step further and write a simple web frontend to demonstrate the use of the endpoint. </p><h2>A minimal web frontend</h2><p>The demo frontend is a plain HTML and JavaScript web app that uses the <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/web/setup#expandable-8\">Firebase Authentication Web SDK</a> and <a href=\"https://github.com/okta/okta-signin-widget\">Okta’s sign-in widget</a> library. </p><p>Start with two containers: one for authenticated user content and one for Okta’s sign-in widget: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">&lt;div id=\"authenticated-user-content\" hidden><br />    &lt;h2>Authenticated with Firebase&lt;/h2><br />    &lt;p id=\"user-info\">&lt;/p><br />    &lt;button onclick=\"firebase.auth().signOut();\">Sign out&lt;/button><br />&lt;/div><br />&lt;div id=\"signin-widget\" hidden>&lt;/div><br /></pre>  <p>Set up a Firebase authentication state listener that shows some user profile information to signed-in users and Okta’s sign-in widget to signed-out users: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">const oktaSignIn = new OktaSignIn({<br />    baseUrl: OKTA_ORG_URL,<br />    redirectUri: window.location.url,<br />    authParams: {<br />        display: 'page',<br />    },<br />    el: '#signin-widget',<br />});<br /><br />firebase.auth().onAuthStateChanged((user) => {<br />    if (user) {<br />        // User is signed in. Display some user profile information.<br />        document.getElementById('user-info').innerHTML =<br />                `Hi, ${user.displayName}! Your email address is<br />                ${user.email} and your UID is ${user.uid}.`;<br />        document.getElementById('authenticated-user-content').hidden = false;<br />        document.getElementById('signin-widget').hidden = true;<br />    } else {<br />        // User is signed out. Display the Okta sign-in widget.<br />        oktaSignIn.showSignInToGetTokens({<br />            clientId: OKTA_CLIENT_ID,<br />            redirectUri: window.location.url,<br />            getAccessToken: true,<br />            getIdToken: true,<br />            scope: 'openid profile email',<br />        });<br />        document.getElementById('authenticated-user-content').hidden = true;<br />        document.getElementById('signin-widget').hidden = false;<br />    }<br />});<br /></pre>  <p>When a user signs in with Okta’s widget, their browser briefly redirects to Okta’s authorization server, and then, assuming the user signed in successfully, redirects back to your app with the response. </p><p>Use Okta’s sign-in library to get the Okta access token from the response and use the access token to get a Firebase custom token from your token exchange endpoint: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">if (oktaSignIn.hasTokensInUrl()) {<br />    // Get the access token from Okta.<br />    const oktaTokenResponse =<br />            await oktaSignIn.authClient.token.parseFromUrl();<br />    const accessToken = oktaTokenResponse.tokens.accessToken.value;<br /><br />    // Use the access token to call the firebaseCustomToken endpoint.<br />    const firebaseTokenResponse = await fetch(CUSTOM_TOKEN_ENDPOINT, {<br />        headers: {<br />            'Authorization': `Bearer ${accessToken}`,<br />        }<br />    });<br />    const firebaseToken = await firebaseTokenResponse.text();<br /><br />    // (Continued below.)<br />}<br /></pre>  <p>And finally, authenticate with Firebase using the custom token: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">// (Continued from above.)<br />try {<br />    await firebase.auth().signInWithCustomToken(firebaseToken);<br />} catch (err) {<br />    console.error('Error signing in with custom token.');<br />}<br /></pre>  <p>When the call to <code>signInWithCustomToken()</code> completes, the auth state listener will detect the change and display the user’s profile information. </p><p>At this point, the user is authenticated with Firebase and you can use any of Firebase’s authentication-enabled services, such as Realtime Database, Cloud Firestore, and Cloud Storage. See the <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/rules\">Security Rules documentation</a> for more information on granting resource access to authenticated users. </p><p>For the complete demo app and backend that the code snippets above came from, see the <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/functions-samples/tree/master/okta-auth\">Authenticate with Firebase using Okta</a> sample on GitHub. Recently, we spoke to Gameloft to understand how they’ve been using Firebase.  </p><b>Why Gameloft turned to Crashlytics </b>  <p>Gameloft is one of the world’s largest gaming studios, employing 4,600 people across 19 global offices. Their vast portfolio consists of over 190 critically-acclaimed, award-winning games that are played by 80 million people each month. Gameloft develops original franchises, like Dungeon Hunter, and also partners with major entertainment studios like Disney.  </p><p>With such a broad portfolio and audience, having the ability to monitor the stability of their games in a holistic way, and the agility to respond to issues quickly, is key to their success. However, they were lacking proper information about crashes, having a hard time reproducing crashes, and unforeseen technical issues were taking too long to track and fix, upsetting both their team and their players.  </p><p>Gameloft realized they needed a robust crash reporting tool that could help them improve game quality in a more streamlined and efficient way, so they turned to Firebase Crashlytics. </p> <b>How Gameloft uses Crashlytics to improve game quality </b>  <p>Crashlytics aggregates crashes by root cause, highlights the impact on players, and provides contextual information on each issue, which helps Gameloft prioritize the crashes to fix first and speeds up their troubleshooting process. By using Crashlytics, Gameloft achieved a 10% reduction in users experiencing crashes <em>overnight </em>for one of their games (Overdrive City)<em>. </em>According to Gameloft, this also led to a bump in the game’s Play Store rating and increased session duration by 16%! +0000","feedId":10592,"bgimg":"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-zqEZcdz7Cvw/XyHu07aDKhI/AAAAAAAAE5s/j4Md2WkagM4G5Alqm1Zqjh_Wrife9B4AgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Partner_Logo_PeopleFun_V2.png","linkMd5":"cbf46d2ddaf19d9cd5ae750804313563","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn82@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-58-34-514_5c8e6c147a4dda23.webp","destWidth":1600,"destHeight":477,"sourceBytes":86647,"destBytes":30546,"author":"Firebase","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-zqEZcdz7Cvw/XyHu07aDKhI/AAAAAAAAE5s/j4Md2WkagM4G5Alqm1Zqjh_Wrife9B4AgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Partner_Logo_PeopleFun_V2.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn82@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-58-34-514_5c8e6c147a4dda23.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-70P5f0fDXDY/XyHtd3BLrqI/AAAAAAAAE5k/FU72OIkD4VMHRwBm0njVwKwIhQKHrsSqACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/0002_LauraWillis320.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn49@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-42-305_e08efd8fcb1fd4cf.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/kBVVctvjhvg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn22@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-58-42-110_040b35e6a37e075b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317114228},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","title":"Manage your Remote Config templates from the Admin Node.js SDK","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/AI2dPg3YAWw/manage-remoteconfig-admin-nodejs-sdk.html","description":"<meta name=\"twitter:image\" content=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-Vvf10YU4amI/XzGUkJ2DWFI/AAAAAAAAE7E/u_DI3HQd-QI-a3qJFxwCtAuHF1k_lmaFgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/remoteconfig.png\"><img style=\"display:none\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-Vvf10YU4amI/XzGUkJ2DWFI/AAAAAAAAE7E/u_DI3HQd-QI-a3qJFxwCtAuHF1k_lmaFgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/remoteconfig.png\"> <figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img style=\"margin:0;\" border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-1DSr4KRpM7k/Xy3DuioGeNI/AAAAAAAAE6s/MBYw-sr7eKIOZo-aUBYg4Im0Okx7LMeZwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Lahiru%2BMaramba.jpg\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div> Lahiru Maramba</div></strong>      <em>Software Engineer</em>  </figcaption></figure>  <p><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/remote-config\">Firebase Remote Config</a> allows developers to control the appearance and the behaviour of their apps without having to publish an app update. You can build your app with in-app default configurations and later change those configurations by updating your project’s Remote Config template. </p><p>We are happy to announce that you can now manage your Remote Config templates directly from the Admin Node.js SDK. </p><p>Typically, you would use the Firebase console to specify and update Remote Config templates manually. However, sometimes you need more automation and server-side control of these settings, such as scheduling Remote Config updates or importing configurations from your own proprietary systems and other Firebase projects. Until now the <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/remote-config/rest\">Remote Config REST API</a> was the only way to implement such use cases, which at times could be cumbersome. </p><p>As of version 8.13.0, the Admin SDK introduced a Remote Config API that simplifies the process of creating, accessing, and updating your Remote Config templates from your own backend servers and also from managed Cloud environments like Google Cloud Functions. </p><p>Let’s go through some examples on how to use the Firebase Admin Node.js SDK to manage your projects’ templates. You can start by creating a fresh Remote Config template for your project, or you can get the current active template and make changes to it. Here's how you can get the current active template in Node.js: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">const admin = require('firebase-admin');<br />admin.initializeApp();<br /><br />const template = await admin.remoteConfig().getTemplate();<br />console.log('ETag from server: ' + template.etag);</pre>  <p>Each Remote Config template version is associated with an entity tag (ETag) for version control purposes. The Remote Config API uses this ETag to prevent race conditions and concurrent updates to resources. To learn more about ETags, see <a href=\"https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/HTTP/Headers/ETag\">ETag - HTTP</a>.  </p><p>Once you have obtained your current active Remote Config template you can start making modifications. Here are some of the operations you can perform using the Admin Node.js SDK: </p><ul> <li>Add new or update existing Remote Config Conditions  <li>Add new or update existing Remote Config Parameters  <li>Manage Remote Config Parameter Groups  <li>Validate your modified Remote Config template  <li>Publish your modified Remote Config template as the current active version </li></ul><p>Let’s go through an example on adding a new Remote Config condition and a parameter to your template. </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">// add a new condition<br />template.conditions.push({<br />  name: 'android_en',<br />  expression: \"device.os == 'android' \"<br />    + \"&& device.country in ['us','uk']\",<br />  tagColor: 'BLUE' as admin.remoteConfig.TagColor,<br />});<br /><br />// add a new parameter, that references the above condition<br />template.parameters['header_text'] = {<br />  defaultValue: {<br />    value: 'A Gryffindor must be brave, talented and helpful.'<br />  },<br />  conditionalValues: {<br />    'android_en': {<br />      value: 'A Droid must be brave, talented and helpful.'<br />    },<br />  },<br />};</pre>  <p>The above example creates a new condition named <code>android_en</code> and uses it in a conditional value of the parameter named <code>header_text</code>. Checkout <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/remote-config/condition-reference\">the documentation</a> to learn more about conditional expressions. Keep in mind that the conditions in a template are listed in descending order by priority. A parameter might have several conditional values associated with it. If multiple conditions evaluate to true, the first condition in the list takes precedence. </p><p>Once you make updates to your template you must publish it to make the new changes effective. After you publish a Remote Config template using the Firebase Admin Node.js SDK, that template becomes the current active version of your project. Before you publish a template you can always validate it to check for any errors. </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">try {<br />  const validatedTemplate = admin.remoteConfig()<br />    .validateTemplate(template);<br />  const publishedTemplate = admin.remoteConfig()<br />    .publishTemplate(validatedTemplate);<br />} catch(err) {<br />  console.log('Error publishing the template:', error);<br />}</pre>  <p>For more details and code samples on programmatically modifying Remote Config templates, check out <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/remote-config/automate-rc\">the detailed documentation</a>. </p><p>In addition to the API for modifying Remote Config templates, the Firebase Admin Node.js SDK provides an API to manage Remote Config template versions. This API supports the following operations. </p><ul> <li>List all stored versions of the Remote Config template  <li>Retrieve a specific version of the Remote Config template  <li>Roll back to a specific stored version of the Remote Config template </li></ul><p>The <code>listVersions</code> operation allows you to retrieve a list of metadata for all stored versions of your project, sorted in reverse chronological order. Note that the last 300 versions are stored. All versions that correspond to non-active Remote Config templates (that is, all except the template that is being fetched by clients) are also deleted if they are more than 90 days old. Here’s a sample code to fetch a list of template versions. </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">// list all the Remote Config template Versions<br />// Only the last 300 versions are stored.<br />const listVersionsResult = admin.remoteConfig().listVersions();<br />listVersionsResult.versions.forEach((version) => {<br />  console.log('version', JSON.stringify(version));<br />});</pre>  <p>For a complete list of available operations and examples, check out <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/remote-config/templates#manage_template_versions_with_the_backend_apis\">the documentation</a> on programmatically managing Remote Config template versions. </p><p>We hope this new addition of the Remote Config API in the Admin Node.js SDK helps with your backend development tasks. Stay tuned for the Remote Config API to be added across other Firebase Admin SDK platforms. We are excited to see how you use the new APIs in your projects! Hop over to our <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/firebase-admin-node\">Github Repo</a> to check out the implementation. Help us further improve our SDKs by providing your valuable feedback, reporting issues, and contributions to our codebase. </p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/AI2dPg3YAWw\" <strong><div>Oleg Kodysh</div></strong>      <em>Developer Relations</em>  </figcaption></figure> <p>In a competitive app ecosystem, making sure your app doesn’t crash frequently is integral to your app’s success. So with the graduation of <a href=\"https://firebase.googleblog.com/2020/06/crashlytics-sdk-now-available.html\">Firebase Crashlytics SDK out of Beta</a>, we think it’s a good time to highlight the benefits of integrating Crashlytics into your app. Read on for a refresher on the essential tools that Crashlytics provides to help you debug crashes and get the most out of your crash reports. </p><h2>1. Debug crashes faster with crash insights</h2><p>Even with access to crash reports, getting to the root cause of a crash can be pretty time consuming. Not only does the Crashlytics dashboard provide a holistic and clear view of what your users are experiencing, but you also get detailed suggestions on what could have caused a fatal error with <strong>crash insights</strong>. </p><p>Crash insights appear on your dashboard next to the crash report and provide additional context by highlighting potential root causes, such as SDK bugs and API misuse, that might be common across multiple apps. This serves as a starting point for investigation, which saves you time and speeds up your workflow.  </p><a href=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-EThIQ05dqPI/Xx90hloXK9I/AAAAAAAAE4o/Bm-yO-6egqA8fzHSB6PXunES-n8RGhsDgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/debug.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-EThIQ05dqPI/Xx90hloXK9I/AAAAAAAAE4o/Bm-yO-6egqA8fzHSB6PXunES-n8RGhsDgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/debug.png\" data-original-width=\"1544\" data-original-height=\"828\" /></a><h2>2. Gain user insights with custom keys and logs</h2><p>It can be frustrating to see a user run into a crash that you can’t seem to reproduce on your end. Crashlytics can help with this by allowing you to track the state and sequence of application usage prior to a crash through <strong><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/customize-crash-reports?platform=android#add-keys\">custom keys</a></strong> and <strong><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/customize-crash-reports?platform=android#add-logs\">custom logs</a></strong>. Custom keys provide a snapshot of information at one point in time, recording the last known value; custom logs record the events a user went through during their session. </p><p>For example, you might want to know how many items a user had in their shopping cart before a crash occurred. By naming a key using a string (e.g. “item purchase count”) and setting the value programmatically, Crashlytics uploads these key/values with the next crash. These keys and values are then visible right next to your stack trace. </p><a href=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-gceRxyDhDPI/Xx91tI3XEZI/AAAAAAAAE4w/XDd9C6oGYZ4JWjxXdcWc2WHaANNJsU_9QCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/keys.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-gceRxyDhDPI/Xx91tI3XEZI/AAAAAAAAE4w/XDd9C6oGYZ4JWjxXdcWc2WHaANNJsU_9QCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/keys.png\" data-original-width=\"802\" data-original-height=\"245\" /></a>Similar to custom keys, custom logs show up within a stack trace in your Crashlytics dashboard. All you need to do is call the Crashlytics logging function and provide your log string, and that custom log is then uploaded with the next crash. Now you can have your state and your logs all in one place. <a href=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-6y8IYlEgEfg/Xx91xbRhnBI/AAAAAAAAE40/TSEjHrrX0hoV3qB5w_mBhPNEQI_NJ-8RQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/logs.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-6y8IYlEgEfg/Xx91xbRhnBI/AAAAAAAAE40/TSEjHrrX0hoV3qB5w_mBhPNEQI_NJ-8RQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/logs.png\" data-original-width=\"800\" data-original-height=\"224\" /></a><h2>3. Reproduce crashes faster with breadcrumbs powered by Google Analytics</h2><p>Even with custom keys and logs, trying to manually capture every event your user triggers in your app can be daunting. However, if you <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/start-using-analytics\">integrate Crashlytics with Google Analytics</a>, you can automatically capture predefined Google Analytics events, known as <strong>breadcrumbs</strong>. Breadcrumbs can further enhance the data captured with custom logs, giving you even more information on the cause of a crash. </p><p>Just like custom logs and keys, breadcrumbs can be found within your stack trace in the Crashlytics dashboard, and will show the actions a user has taken prior to a crash, as well as the parameters within the event. </p><p>For instance, going back to the shopping cart example, breadcrumbs will capture event parameters like product ID, product name, type of currency used, quantity of items in the cart, etc. Here is a full list of the <a href=\"https://support.google.com/firebase/answer/6317485?hl=en\">automatically collected events</a> that Google Analytics breadcrumbs captures. </p><a href=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-g9i_H1X4mew/Xx92Lusq_bI/AAAAAAAAE5A/OYfNCYK9doQjYbX1e_AK_aLcEh7-eBCogCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Screen%2BShot%2B2020-07-27%2Bat%2B5.49.42%2BPM.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-g9i_H1X4mew/Xx92Lusq_bI/AAAAAAAAE5A/OYfNCYK9doQjYbX1e_AK_aLcEh7-eBCogCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Screen%2BShot%2B2020-07-27%2Bat%2B5.49.42%2BPM.png\" data-original-width=\"761\" data-original-height=\"243\" /></a><h2>4. Receive notifications in real-time with Crashlytics alerts</h2><p>You never want to miss a critical user issue, but it can be tough to stay on top of crash reports around-the-clock. Using <strong>Crashlytics alerts,</strong> you can configure real-time alerts by three different levels of your app’s stability. <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/velocity-alerts\">Velocity alerts,</a> considered high priority, are sent when an issue goes over a certain threshold within your user base. Regression alerts are sent when a previously closed issue has recurred in a new version of your app, typically medium priority. New issue alerts are sent when a new issue has occurred, and are generally low priority. </p><p>You can customize these alerts in the Crashlytics console, and receive them via Slack, PagerDuty, Jira, or email. </p><center><a href=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-cfy2aVjUrtI/Xx92i0wTp7I/AAAAAAAAE5I/BfRceuqtYGYNO0D---pcUbQSaWl9HmLUACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" style=\"width:80%\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-cfy2aVjUrtI/Xx92i0wTp7I/AAAAAAAAE5I/BfRceuqtYGYNO0D---pcUbQSaWl9HmLUACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase.png\" data-original-width=\"609\" data-original-height=\"765\" /></a></center><h2>5. Segment crash data with BigQuery and visualize it with Data Studio</h2><p>Not only can you view your crashes in the Crashlytics dashboard, but you can also <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/bigquery-export\">export all Crashlytics data to BigQuery</a>. This enables you to filter and segment your user data for further analysis. For example, you can figure out emerging crashes in new code, or see the top Issues for today so you can prioritize and fix them faster. </p><p>You can also use our <a href=\"https://datastudio.google.com/c/reporting/10TMAKxL0ZxcNGTLDQy1LAF5V7uNDYxRC/page/1xZU/preview\">Data Studio template</a> to easily visualize this data with custom dashboards. Data Studio dashboards are easy to collaborate on and share so your team can work more efficiently; even your team members who aren't comfortable with SQL can easily maneuver BigQuery data sets. </p><a href=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-CikJWUaJVto/Xx929v3scZI/AAAAAAAAE5Q/YafD0J8ZJ4Ay9VTSZyJOFf9kWXvL_Fk2wCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebasecrashlytics.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-CikJWUaJVto/Xx929v3scZI/AAAAAAAAE5Q/YafD0J8ZJ4Ay9VTSZyJOFf9kWXvL_Fk2wCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebasecrashlytics.png\" data-original-width=\"1201\" data-original-height=\"895\" /></a><p>And recently we also <a href=\"https://twitter.com/firebase/status/1256328237308710914\">launched the ability to export this data in real time</a>, enabling you to power custom workflows and alerts based on real-time data. </p><p>These are just a few examples of the exciting things you can do with Crashlytics to keep your apps stable and your users happy. As always, if you need help <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/get-started?platform=unity\">getting started</a> please feel free to reach out to us directly through our <a href=\"https://firebase.community/\">Community Slack</a> or via <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/questions/tagged/crashlytics\">Stack Overflow</a>! </p><img game","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/lR53-ofqWyw/google-loteria-realtime-database.html","description":"<meta name=\"twitter:image\" content=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-b-kqsGGv6pc/Xi9S8KatpDI/AAAAAAAAEo8/x7gfcfk_H2MUCwfoFsjXYTtn0bYGUuQwQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/loteria.gif\"><img style=\"display:none\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-b-kqsGGv6pc/Xi9S8KatpDI/AAAAAAAAEo8/x7gfcfk_H2MUCwfoFsjXYTtn0bYGUuQwQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/loteria.gif\"> <figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uikGz92Glt4/Xi9SR805MtI/AAAAAAAAEow/H1smJWceoAARutz0wAcOt3HNZMaBUBryACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Screen%2BShot%2B2020-01-27%2Bat%2B1.12.23%2BPM.png\"style=\"margin: 0 0 0 0;\"  />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div>Jacob Howcraft</div></strong>      <em>Engineer, Doodle Team</em>  </figcaption></figure><p>Did you play the interactive Google Doodle for the Mexican card game “Lotería” last December? If not, you can still <a href=\"https://www.google.com/doodles/celebrating-loteria\">check it out here</a>. Lotería is a multiplayer bingo-style game that supports up to five players per room. In the digitized Doodle version, you can play with random people around the world or create a private game with your friends.  </p><p>The Lotería game was developed in JavaScript primarily by one engineer over a few months, and the game's usage jumped from no players to millions literally overnight. The multiplayer component that supported it all was built entirely with the Firebase Realtime Database, which meant we could spend more time developing a great user experience, and less time worrying about infrastructure. Let's look into how we did it! </p><h2>Gameplay</h2>Lotería is a card game similar to bingo, where each player has their own board. A dealer calls out cards, and players check their own boards for a match. If they have the card, they mark it⁠—traditionally with a pinto bean. When a player matches the win pattern for that game, such as a full row or column, they call out, “Lotería!”. The first person to call “Lotería!” wins. <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-b-kqsGGv6pc/Xi9S8KatpDI/AAAAAAAAEo8/x7gfcfk_H2MUCwfoFsjXYTtn0bYGUuQwQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/loteria.gif\" data-original-width=\"800\" data-original-height=\"450\" alt=\"Loteria game animation\"/>There are two parts of the game that need to happen in real-time for all players. The first is dealing out the cards, and the second is when somebody calls, “Lotería!”. For the dealer, we randomly choose one of the players to act as the game host and run the dealer logic. On a repeating interval, the dealer randomly selects a new card from the deck and writes it to a location in the database that all the other players are listening to. We also include the time that the deal started, and the milliseconds until the next deal. Each client can use these numbers to show an animated timer. So the data looks like this: <p><code>/games/{gameId}/deal = {card, time, countDown}</code></p><p>To detect the winner, all players listen to another database location at <code>/games/{gameId}/winner. </code> When a player clicks “Lotería!” and has a valid winning pattern on their board, we write their ID to that location. </p><p>Often, there’s a close tie, so we use <code>Reference.transaction</code> to ensure that only the first player to call, “Lotería!” is declared the winner. We do this by making sure that <code>/games/{gameId}/winner</code> doesn’t already have an existing value before we write anything to that location. </p><h2>Matchmaking</h2><p>To create random public games, we utilized Cloud Functions for Firebase in conjunction with the Realtime Database. </p><p>When a player selects “Random Match,” their player ID is written to the location <code>/queue/</code>. A Cloud Function is set up to trigger when a new entry is created, and it checks whether we have enough players to start a game. If so, it creates the game at<code> /games/<em>{gameId}</em></code>, and writes the gameId to each player's entry, like so: <code>/players/<em>{playerId}</em>/gameId = <em>{gameId}</em></code>   <p>Players listen to <code>gameId</code> on their own database entries, so they know when to transition to the gameplay screen. </p><img border=\"0\" id=\"imgFull\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-laNA4-7JwBk/Xi9TsYexFCI/AAAAAAAAEpI/zyGtz95BYk0rn9K1OZq6Y07hfRClUoaBACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/matchmaking.gif\" data-original-width=\"480\" data-original-height=\"270\"alt=\"matchmaking\"/><p>Since there are a lot of players connecting simultaneously, and the Cloud Function is asynchronous, we once again used a transaction to ensure that players are removed from the queue and placed into games atomically. Making one game at a time atomically could create a bottleneck, so instead we create as many games as possible in one transaction. </p><h2>Scaling</h2><p>Interactive Google Doodles get millions of players, so we needed to make sure that the database could handle the load. To do this, we <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/database/usage/sharding\">sharded the database into multiple instances</a>. Each instance can handle a limited amount of traffic, so we estimated how much traffic we expected to have. To do this, we wrote a load testing bot that could be run repeatedly and measured how much load this generated on a single instance. Then, we extrapolated that number to how many players we expected, which then gave us the number of instances we might need. </p><p>However, there is a downside to having too many instances: if players are spread too thinly, it might take longer for enough to connect to one instance to start a game. To solve this problem, we created an entry in the default database instance called “shards.” This was a number that could be adjusted from 1 to the maximum number of shards we created. Clients read this value and only used shards in that range. Since the Doodle was only live on the Google homepage for a short time, we updated the shards value manually through the Firebase Console, setting it lower when there was less traffic, and higher when there were spikes. Naturally, this process could be automated for a longer-running application. </p><h2>More time on gameplay, less time on infrastructure</h2>Lotería is a game that has a lot of significance for many members of the team, and we were proud to make an experience that shares our love of the game with the rest of the world. Building our game with help from the Realtime Database meant that we could get our backend up and running quickly, without having to worry about scaling. We hope to use it again in future Doodles and encourage you to give it a try for powering your own multiplayer game.   <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/lR53-ofqWyw\" +0000","feedId":10592,"bgimg":"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-b-kqsGGv6pc/Xi9S8KatpDI/AAAAAAAAEo8/x7gfcfk_H2MUCwfoFsjXYTtn0bYGUuQwQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/loteria.gif","linkMd5":"cd8b37ed9d7a648ac08b6509ea463d71","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn74@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-39-846_fc776677f62bd399.webp","destWidth":800,"destHeight":450,"sourceBytes":481785,"destBytes":764812,"author":"Firebase","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-b-kqsGGv6pc/Xi9S8KatpDI/AAAAAAAAEo8/x7gfcfk_H2MUCwfoFsjXYTtn0bYGUuQwQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/loteria.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn74@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-39-846_fc776677f62bd399.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uikGz92Glt4/Xi9SR805MtI/AAAAAAAAEow/H1smJWceoAARutz0wAcOt3HNZMaBUBryACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Screen%2BShot%2B2020-01-27%2Bat%2B1.12.23%2BPM.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn61@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-45-119_aba52cafef7108b8.webp","https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-laNA4-7JwBk/Xi9TsYexFCI/AAAAAAAAEpI/zyGtz95BYk0rn9K1OZq6Y07hfRClUoaBACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/matchmaking.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn2@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-58-44-112_7bc3b1b09bfc5585.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/lR53-ofqWyw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn82@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-58-44-560_ef6bd727645eb0e9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317114229},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 Translate","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/pU_BgJXVTUo/auto-translate-firebase-in-app-messages.html","description":"<figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-YBsE36eeuAg/XiI_M3WYy2I/AAAAAAAAEl4/ZXRXJjII5yUfmmn3MeRngv438k5cteUbwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase%2Bauthor.jpg\" style=\"margin: 0 0 0 0;\" data-original-width=\"600\" data-original-height=\"600\" />\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div><a href=\"https://twitter.com/cpsloal\">Ali Satter</a></div></strong>      <em>Product Manager</em>  </figcaption></figure> <p>It's been over a year since we first announced <a href=\"https://firebase.googleblog.com/2018/08/introducing-firebase-in-app-messaging.html\">Firebase In-App Messaging</a> to help you guide app users who are <em>actively using your app</em> by sending them targeted and contextual messages.  In-App Messaging allows you to communicate with your most valuable users and deepen their engagement by surfacing relevant offers, information, and tips as they use your app. </p><p>We have been actively working on adding more features and expanding on the capabilities of Firebase In-App messaging (such as new message layouts), and we’re excited to highlight another new feature today - the ability to auto-translate your message into multiple languages using Google Translate. </p><p>As your app user base expands into more countries and languages, it's important to make sure messages are localized for each recipient to improve engagement, and for that we have added an easy way to translate your message to many languages.  To get started is simple.   </p><p><strong>1. First choose your message style and content. </strong>  </p><p><a href=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-UdAm9pcseOM/XiJHdxBzDBI/AAAAAAAAEmw/fyAT0Z5fLxUyxoVBQqepuxHPngYjIYu5wCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/FB1%2B%25281%2529.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-UdAm9pcseOM/XiJHdxBzDBI/AAAAAAAAEmw/fyAT0Z5fLxUyxoVBQqepuxHPngYjIYu5wCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/FB1%2B%25281%2529.png\" data-original-width=\"842\" data-original-height=\"1229\" /></a></p><p><strong>2. Set your target criteria</strong></p><p>Then in the targeting section, when you select your app, Firebase will look at your audience and determine if localizing message content would help, and provide you a helpful nudge to provide localized content: </p><p><a href=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-c0tkCHAD8LE/XiJHhBTyZEI/AAAAAAAAEm0/4OSh2PRW7TI1lACu_cehfOUN0Y7YSFLbQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Fb2%2B%25281%2529.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-c0tkCHAD8LE/XiJHhBTyZEI/AAAAAAAAEm0/4OSh2PRW7TI1lACu_cehfOUN0Y7YSFLbQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Fb2%2B%25281%2529.png\" data-original-width=\"842\" data-original-height=\"676\" /></a></p><p><strong>3. Click on <em>Localize</em>, and you will see the following dialog.</strong></p><p>The first section of the dialog contains the default language and strings to be localized.  And if you would like to provide alternative translated messages, click on Localize next to the language you would like. </p><p><a href=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-Vq7Hmb9ZVwY/XiJHkyxscgI/AAAAAAAAEm4/bKau6C9pJ0gf4adyACcBwcp6EsgpsHKQACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/fb3%2B%25281%2529.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-Vq7Hmb9ZVwY/XiJHkyxscgI/AAAAAAAAEm4/bKau6C9pJ0gf4adyACcBwcp6EsgpsHKQACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/fb3%2B%25281%2529.png\" data-original-width=\"583\" data-original-height=\"729\" /></a></p><p>When you click on Localize, you can either enter the localized message yourself, or click on the <strong><em>Translate</em></strong> button and let Google Translate automatically do the translation for you. </p><p><a href=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-QX0nsR20sRo/XiJHLhf45cI/AAAAAAAAEmo/8EwYcKRd5xITAZ20_sCa64Wo4qEH32RHgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase.corp.google.com_u_0_project_primer-api_inappmessaging_compose%2B%25286%2529%2Bcopy.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-QX0nsR20sRo/XiJHLhf45cI/AAAAAAAAEmo/8EwYcKRd5xITAZ20_sCa64Wo4qEH32RHgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase.corp.google.com_u_0_project_primer-api_inappmessaging_compose%2B%25286%2529%2Bcopy.png\" data-original-width=\"583\" data-original-height=\"729\" /></a></p><p><strong>4. Save your translations</strong></p><p>Once you are done with all the translations, click on <strong>Save</strong> and continue completing the rest of the steps for your messaging campaign. </p><p>Unless you provide localized messages, all your users - irregardless of their locale - will receive the message in the default language.  If you provide translations, users who’s locale matches available translations will get the localized message, and everyone else will get the default message. </p><p>We have a lot more product updates in the works for this year, and look forward to sharing them with you. If you are not already using Firebase In-App Messaging, then 2020 is the year to start. Get started today by checking out the documentation: </p><p><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/in-app-messaging/get-started?platform=android\">Getting started with Firebase In-App Messaging</a></p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/pU_BgJXVTUo\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 21 Jan 2020 17:50:00 +0000","feedId":10592,"bgimg":"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-YBsE36eeuAg/XiI_M3WYy2I/AAAAAAAAEl4/ZXRXJjII5yUfmmn3MeRngv438k5cteUbwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase%2Bauthor.jpg","linkMd5":"d982acc9c3dc03baca2e23f0819e28bd","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn98@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-58-34-482_80047222986ae22b.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":600,"sourceBytes":38886,"destBytes":16624,"author":"Firebase","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-YBsE36eeuAg/XiI_M3WYy2I/AAAAAAAAEl4/ZXRXJjII5yUfmmn3MeRngv438k5cteUbwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase%2Bauthor.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn98@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-58-34-482_80047222986ae22b.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-UdAm9pcseOM/XiJHdxBzDBI/AAAAAAAAEmw/fyAT0Z5fLxUyxoVBQqepuxHPngYjIYu5wCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/FB1%2B%25281%2529.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn98@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-44-651_dcf8b4b1636d8dbb.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-c0tkCHAD8LE/XiJHhBTyZEI/AAAAAAAAEm0/4OSh2PRW7TI1lACu_cehfOUN0Y7YSFLbQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Fb2%2B%25281%2529.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn90@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-58-43-480_bac9fb5ee31a5697.webp","https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-Vq7Hmb9ZVwY/XiJHkyxscgI/AAAAAAAAEm4/bKau6C9pJ0gf4adyACcBwcp6EsgpsHKQACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/fb3%2B%25281%2529.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn82@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-58-42-245_eccc6a6f1552c448.webp","https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-QX0nsR20sRo/XiJHLhf45cI/AAAAAAAAEmo/8EwYcKRd5xITAZ20_sCa64Wo4qEH32RHgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/firebase.corp.google.com_u_0_project_primer-api_inappmessaging_compose%2B%25286%2529%2Bcopy.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn6@2020_4/2020/08/25/00-58-44-760_0c36370a72cc8f52.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/pU_BgJXVTUo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn78@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-58-42-112_4f06b0c66a5570f3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317114229},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","title":" New API for manually tracking screen views in Google Analytics","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/h1yqpWD0-Ec/google-analytics-manual-screen-view.html","description":"<meta name=\"twitter:image\" content=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png\"><img style=\"display:none\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png\"> <figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Ex3KcgLH91Q/XJAqKhdbuhI/AAAAAAAADbM/mnv4itWPldUKppT4rMahJ_ONi1nsH8XxQCLcBGAs/s1600/unnamed.jpg\" style=\"margin:0 0 0 0%; max-height:100%;\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div>Sumit Chandel</div></strong>      <em>Developer Advocate</em>  </figcaption></figure><center><a href=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" style=\"width:100%\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png\" data-original-width=\"1050\" data-original-height=\"611\" /></a></center><p>The latest Google Analytics for Firebase SDK release now makes it possible for you to <strong>log the<code> screen_view</code> event manually </strong>for both Android and iOS! </p><p>When you add the Google Analytics for Firebase SDK to your app, there are a number of different events that <a href=\"https://support.google.com/firebase/answer/6317485?hl=en\">start getting collected automatically</a> - including the <code>screen_view</code> event for your Android and iOS apps. </p><p>This event is incredibly important to understand your users' behavior since it can tell you the number of users who have visited each screen in your app, and which screens are the most popular. In addition, this event powers the <a href=\"https://support.google.com/analytics/answer/9143382\">Behavior reports offered in Google Analytics’s App + Web properties, </a> which can tell you how much time users spend in each screen. It also powers the <a href=\"https://support.google.com/analytics/answer/9317498?hl=en\">Path Analysis tool</a> where you can discover the most popular navigational paths within your app.  </p><a href=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-JVLCzNVargs/XzsDBbQQ8uI/AAAAAAAAE8o/V1NplYm2Q6QqR3o_AQ69kuUjpENEUnROQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/behavioroverview.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-JVLCzNVargs/XzsDBbQQ8uI/AAAAAAAAE8o/V1NplYm2Q6QqR3o_AQ69kuUjpENEUnROQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/behavioroverview.png\" data-original-width=\"1600\" data-original-height=\"1192\" /></a><p id=\"imgCaption\">Behavior overview reporting page</p><a href=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-CkdS4k13D6g/XzsDNaNV61I/AAAAAAAAE8s/0CGZVUfPS1E_i7gaJGl_e9Lzm7RdzfzoACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/pathanalysis.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-CkdS4k13D6g/XzsDNaNV61I/AAAAAAAAE8s/0CGZVUfPS1E_i7gaJGl_e9Lzm7RdzfzoACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/pathanalysis.png\" data-original-width=\"1470\" data-original-height=\"1094\" /></a><p id=\"imgCaption\">Path analysis technique</p><p>Most of the automatically collected events, including<code> screen_view</code>, use protected event names that prevent you from being able to manually log events with the same name. There are good reasons for doing this because a lot of these events power other Firebase products, like A/B Testing and Firebase Predictions, and first class reporting features in Google Analytics. </p><p>But we’ve heard a lot of your feedback - and <code>screen_view</code> is one event for which you wanted more control over how and when the event gets logged - in addition to having the option to continue logging events automatically. </p><p>So our latest release now makes this possible. This release also marks the deprecation of the <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/android/com/google/firebase/analytics/FirebaseAnalytics#setCurrentScreen(android.app.Activity,%20java.lang.String,%20java.lang.String)\">setCurrentScreen</a> (on Android) and <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/ios/firebaseanalytics/api/reference/Classes/FIRAnalytics#+setscreenname:screenclass:\">setScreenName</a> (on iOS) methods which will be replaced by the new calls to manually log the <code>screen_view</code> event. </p><p>There are many benefits to having more control for when the screen_view event gets logged:<br></p><ol> <li><b>Only log screen_view events that are meaningful in your app</b><br></br><p>For example, you might only want to log a<code> screen_view</code> event when a user has spent at least some minimum amount of time on the screen. <br></br><p>In fact, you might recall that Google Analytics <a href=\"https://firebase.googleblog.com/2018/12/new-changes-sessions-user-engagement.html\">previously logged session_start events ten seconds after an app first loaded</a> into the foreground. While this has changed and <code>session_start</code> events are logged immediately, there can be instances in your app when it makes sense to wait a few seconds before logging a meaningful <code>screen_view</code>.<br></br><p>In a video streaming app, users may often load up a title selection screen and quickly scroll through and select a series they’ve already been watching to pick up where they left off. They might not really be searching for new titles, even though they’re visiting the title screen, and you'll want to only log screen views here when you really know that they’re looking for new titles, not just quickly scrolling through it. </li><li><b>Log screen_view events for important sections and subscreens in your app</b><br></br><p>Your app might have subsections in some screens (think “Container View Controllers” with inner Child VCs or Fragments), or in the case of a game or a Flutter app, be driven by one screen that powers other views that present meaningful actions a user can take, or represent places in your app where users will spend a significant amount of time. In such cases, you might want to log a <code>screen_view</code> event manually, and add additional event parameters that represent the subsections of your app that your users spend more time exploring. </p><p>A good example here is an app that provides a customer support chat interface that overlays onto an existing screen the user is viewing. In such cases, you might want to log a manual <code>screen_view</code> event that represents that chat interface and that can collect more information in added event parameters - like which topic or which screen the user was on before they opened the support chat window. </p></li></ol><p>In addition to the reasons above, there was an <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/firebase-ios-sdk/issues/1894\">issue on iOS devices</a> with automatically collected <code>screen_view</code> events using the soon-to-be-deprecated <code>setScreenName</code> method in which the events were dual-logged. This change also corrects this issue so it is no longer a factor. </p><h2>How to manually log a screen_view event</h2><p>You can log a manual <code>screen_view</code> just like you would any other event in Google Analytics. You can also include the two optional parameters (i) <code>screen_name</code> and (ii) <code>screen_class</code>, as well as any custom event parameters you want to include. The two optional parameters take the place of the <code>firebase_screen</code> and <code>firebase_screen_class</code> event parameters that get passed into automatically collected screen_view events. </p><p>Specifically for iOS, this would look like: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">override func viewDidAppear(_ animated: Bool) {<br />    super.viewDidAppear(animated)<br /><br />    // After enough time has passed to make this screen view significant.<br />    Analytics.logEvent(AnalyticsEventScreenView, parameters: [<br />        AnalyticsParameterScreenName: screenName!,<br />        AnalyticsParameterScreenClass: screenClass!,<br />        MyAppAnalyticsParameterFitnessCategory: category!<br />    ])<br />}<br /></pre>  <p>And for Android: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">@Override<br />public void onResume() {<br />    super.onResume();<br /><br />    // After enough time has passed to make this screen view significant.<br />    Bundle bundle = new Bundle();<br />    bundle.putString(FirebaseAnalytics.Param.SCREEN_NAME, screenName);<br />    bundle.putString(FirebaseAnalytics.Param.SCREEN_CLASS, screenClass);<br />    bundle.putString(MyAppAnalyticsConstants.Param.TOPIC, topic);<br />    mFirebaseAnalytics.logEvent(FirebaseAnalytics.Event.SCREEN_VIEW, bundle);<br />}<br /></pre>  <p>As mentioned before, the new API will allow you to log manual <code>screen_view</code> events along with the option to continue collecting <code>screen_view</code> events automatically. Since both are possible at the same time, let’s get into some questions to learn more about how the new feature works with the existing automatic event collection. </p>  <h3>Can I disable automatically collected <code>screen_view</code> events and only use manual logging?</h3>  <p>Yes. While you can use both together at the same time, you can disable automatic event collection for <code>screen_view</code> events if you’d like. On iOS, set  <code>FirebaseAutomaticScreenReportingEnabled</code> to “NO” in your info.plist. On Android, set <code>google_analytics_automatic_screen_reporting_enabled</code> to “false” in your manifest. </p> <h3>What changes do I need to make to migrate from the setScreenName / setCurrentScreen method calls?</h3>  <p>The <code>setScreenName</code> and <code>setCurrentScreen</code> method calls were used to set a custom-defined screen name as the <code>firebase_screen_name</code> parameter value included with all automatically logged <code>screen_view</code> events. If you want to continue collecting custom screen names on your <code>screen_view</code> events, you will need to log the <code>screen_view</code> event manually and pass in the <code>screen_name</code> event parameter (likely using the same value you were using in the soon-to-be deprecated methods). </p><p>Note that for automatically reported <code>screen_view</code> events, the screen name value will be left blank, and the screen class value will be set to that of the most recently shown screen. </p><p>If you disable automatic screen reporting, some values are still accessible and can be automatically added to your manually logged <code>screen_view</code> events, such as the <code>firebase_screen_class</code> parameter. </p><p>This is because parameters like the screen class are first-class metrics that feed into Google Analytics’ Behavior Reports, and so we’ve gone the extra mile to automatically populate this field using information we know on the client.  On Android, the current activity will be used to populate the screen class parameter. On iOS, if you make a call to the<code> logEvent()</code> method, the root UIViewController will be used to populate the screen class value. You can always overwrite this value by passing in a different <code>screen_class</code> parameter, though. On both platforms the screen name will be left blank (unless you specify it), and the value “not set” will be displayed in the Google Analytics console. </p><h3>Do other event parameters that get passed into automatically collected <code>screen_view</code> events also get passed into manually logged <code>screen_view</code> events?</h3> <p>Yes, all event parameters that are reported along with automatically collected screen_view events, such as <code>engagement_time_msec</code>, and <code>firebase_previous_screen</code>  will also be automatically reported with manually logged screen_view events, besides the special cases for the values for screen name and screen class described in the section above. </p><p>We hope you enjoy the new ability to manually log your screen view events, and would love to hear more feedback from you about this feature, or about the Google Analytics for Firebase SDK for your apps in general. Please reach out to us at our <a href=\"https://groups.google.com/g/firebase-talk\">Firebase Google Group</a>, on <a href=\"https://stackoverflow.com/questions/tagged/firebase\">StackOverflow</a> or on our <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/support/troubleshooter/report/features\">Firebase Support channel</a> to submit your feature requests. </p><img +0000","feedId":10592,"bgimg":"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png","linkMd5":"10419dc52174ee5776741776f6effd03","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn6@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-58-34-477_1b32792676faa444.webp","destWidth":1050,"destHeight":611,"sourceBytes":42933,"destBytes":16246,"author":"Firebase","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Z3vnqpbNNOI/XzsCjKC5haI/AAAAAAAAE8g/cy7J373JRicTBnW5HUooQRIRaDpAFPBXgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/blog_post_image.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn6@2020_3/2020/08/25/00-58-34-477_1b32792676faa444.webp","https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Ex3KcgLH91Q/XJAqKhdbuhI/AAAAAAAADbM/mnv4itWPldUKppT4rMahJ_ONi1nsH8XxQCLcBGAs/s1600/unnamed.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn6@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-58-43-575_d6be73ff5af6ed39.webp","https://4.bp.blogspot.com/-JVLCzNVargs/XzsDBbQQ8uI/AAAAAAAAE8o/V1NplYm2Q6QqR3o_AQ69kuUjpENEUnROQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/behavioroverview.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn2@2020_6/2020/08/25/00-58-43-178_78c936f27c12153e.webp","https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-CkdS4k13D6g/XzsDNaNV61I/AAAAAAAAE8s/0CGZVUfPS1E_i7gaJGl_e9Lzm7RdzfzoACLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/pathanalysis.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn2@2020_5/2020/08/25/00-58-44-862_ceb24cc5bd4ea507.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/h1yqpWD0-Ec":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn33@2020_2/2020/08/25/00-58-42-116_108e6a0a08833fba.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598317114229},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 08:58:34","title":"New improvements to Firestore Security Rules","link":"http://feedproxy.google.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~3/IRFyi2Uxwx0/new-firestore-security-rules-features.html","description":"<meta name=\"Code snippet\"><img style=\"display:none;\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-mgKVS5D-2W4/XupeRC3Z4TI/AAAAAAAAEzg/Tw1cD41eQKELSZBSsgIjzWRBSm9oPbsgwCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/Screen%2BShot%2B2020-06-09%2Bat%2B3.48.03%2BPM.png\">  <figure class=\"profile\"> <div class=\"profile-picture\">    <img style=\"margin:0;\" border=\"0\" src=\"https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-deycj3QmJ_o/Wsum3yaKqaI/AAAAAAAAB_s/2ufTvigOiIMXRP3iTU7wRGuzmirUTpWjACLcBGAs/s1600/209641.jpeg\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div>Rachel Myers (@rachelmyers on Twitter)</div></strong>      <em>Developer Programs Engineer</em>  </figcaption></figure>   <p>If you're using Cloud Firestore or Cloud Storage for Firebase, you're also using Security Rules. (If you're using the default rules instead of tailoring them to your app, this is where to <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/firestore/security/get-started\">start</a>!) We're excited to announce that in the last few months we've released some substantial improvements to the tools for writing and debugging Rules, improvements to the Rules language itself, and increases to the size limits for Rules!. These are a few of the great new features. Check out the <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/support/release-notes/security-rules\">Security Rules Release Notes</a> for a comprehensive list of everything we've released. </p><h3>Sets</h3>  <p>We've released several improvements to make the rules language more expressive and succinct. One particularly verbose pattern was comparing the new values of a document to existing values. The new <code><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/rules/rules.Set\">Set type</a></code> available in Rules is purpose-built for these comparisons, and also has methods for functionality you'd expect for a Set, like getting the intersection, union, or difference between Sets. For examples:  <p>Allow a user to create a document if the document has required and optional fields, but not others: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">allow create: if (request.resource.data.keys().toSet()<br />  .hasOnly([\"required\",\"and\",\"optional\",\"keys\"])</pre>  <p>Sets come with <code>==</code> and <code>in</code> operators and <code>hasAll</code>, <code>hasAny</code>, <code>hasOnly</code>, <code>difference</code>, <code>intersection</code>, <code>union</code>, and <code>size</code> methods.  </p><h3>New Map methods</h3>  <p>Sets are most useful in conjunction with the <code><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/rules/rules.Map\">Map</a></code> class, and because the <code>request</code> and <code>resource</code> objects are both structured as maps, you're probably already familiar with it. <code>Map</code> recently got a few new methods, <code>diff</code> and <code>get</code>, that will hopefully open the door to more concise rules for everyone. Here's how they work:  <p>Map.diff() is called on one map, and takes the second map as an argument: <code>map1.diff(map2)</code>. It returns a <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/rules/rules.MapDiff\">MapDiff</a> object, and all of the MapDiff methods, like <code>addedKeys</code>, <code>changedKeys</code>, or <code>affectedKeys</code> return a <code>Set</code> object.  </p><p>Map.diff() can solve some verbose patterns like checking which fields changed before and after a request. For example, this rule allows an update if the \"maxLevel\" field was the only field changed: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">allow update: if  <br />  request.resource.data.diff(resource.data).changedKeys().hasOnly([\"maxLevel\"]);<br /></pre>  <p>In the next example, posts have a field indicating the user role required to modify the post. We'll use Map.get() to get the <code>\"roleToEdit\"</code> field. If the document doesn't have the field, it will default to the <code>\"admin\"</code> role. Then we'll compare that to the role that's on the user's custom claims: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">allow update, delete: if resource.data.get(\"roleToEdit\", \"admin\") == request.auth.token.role;</pre>  <p>Keep in mind that because Sets are not ordered but Lists are. You can convert a List to a Set, but you can't convert a Set to a List. </p><h3>Local variables</h3>  <p><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/rules/rules-language#function\">Local variables</a> have been one of the most requested features in Rules, and they're now available within functions. You can declare a variable using the keyword <code>let</code>, and you can have up to 10 local variables per function. </p><p>Say you're commonly checking that a user meets the same three conditions before granting access: that they're an owner of the product or an admin user, that they successfully answered a challenge question, and that they meet the karma threshold.  </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">rules_version = '2';<br />service cloud.firestore {<br />  match /databases/{database}/documents {<br />    match /products/{product} {<br />      allow read: if true;<br />      allow write: if (exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/admins/$(request.auth.uid))<br />      || exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/product/owner/$(request.auth.uid)))<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.passChallenge == true<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.karma > 5;<br />    }<br />    match /categories/{category} {<br />      allow read: if true;<br />      allow write: if (exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/admins/$(request.auth.uid))<br />      || exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/product/owner/$(request.auth.uid)))<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.passChallenge == true<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.karma > 5;<br />    }<br />    match /brands/{brand} {<br />      allow read, write: if (exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/admins/$(request.auth.uid))<br />      || exists(/databases/$(database)/documents/product/owner/$(request.auth.uid)))<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.passChallenge == true<br />      && get(/databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(request.auth.uid))<br />         .data.karma > 5;<br />    }<br />  }<br />}</pre>  <p>Those conditions, along with the paths I'm using for lookups can all now become variables in a function, which creates more readable rules: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">rules_version = '2';<br />service cloud.firestore {<br />  match /databases/{database}/documents {<br />  <br />    function privilegedAccess(uid, product) {<br />      let adminDatabasePath = /databases/$(database)/documents/admins/$(uid);<br />      let userDatabasePath = /databases/$(database)/documents/users/$(uid);<br />      let ownerDatabasePath = /databases/$(database)/documents/$(product)/owner/$(uid);  <br /><br />      let isOwnerOrAdmin = exists(adminDatabasePath) || exists(ownerDatabasePath);<br />      let meetsChallenge = get(userDatabasePath).data.get(\"passChallenge\", false) == true;<br />      let meetsKarmaThreshold = get(userDatabasePath).get(\"karma\", 1) > 5;<br />      return  isOwnerOrAdmin && meetsChallenge && meetsKarmaThreshold;<br />    }<br /><br />    match /products/{product} {<br />      allow read: if true;<br />      allow write: if privilegedAccess();<br />    }<br />    <br />    match /categories/{category} {<br />      allow read: if true;<br />      allow write: if privilegedAccess();<br />    }<br />    <br />    match /brands/{brand} {<br />      allow read, write: if privilegedAccess();<br />    }<br />  }<br />}</pre>  <p>You can see at a glance that the same conditions grant access to write to documents in the three different collections.  </p><p>The updated version also uses <code><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/rules/rules.Map#get\">map.get()</a></code> to fetch the <code>karma</code> and <code>passChallenge </code>fields from the user data, which helps keep the new function concise. In this example, if there is no <code>karma</code> field for a user, then the <code>get</code> returns false. Keep in mind that <code>Map.get()</code> fetches a specific field, and is separate from the <code><a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/reference/js/firebase.firestore.DocumentReference#get\">DocumentReference.get()</a></code> that fetches a document.  <h3>Ternary operator</h3>  <p>This is the first time we've introduced an <code>if/else </code>control flow, and we hope it will make rules smoother and more powerful. </p><p>Here's an example of using a ternary operator to specify complex conditions for a write. A user can update a document in two cases: first, if they're an admin user, they need to either set the field <code>overrideReason</code> or <code>approvedBy</code>. Second, if they're not an admin user, then the update must include all the required fields: </p>   <pre class=\"prettyprint\">allow update: if isAdminUser(request.auth.uid) ? <br />  request.resource.data.keys().toSet().hasAny([\"overrideReason\", \"approvedBy\"]) :<br />  request.resource.data.keys().toSet().hasAll([\"all\", \"the\", \"required\", \"fields\"])</pre>  <p>It was possible to express this before the ternary, but this is a much more concise expression. </p><h3>The size limit for rules files is now 256 KB!</h3>  <p>And finally, here's a feature for those of you with longer rules. Until now, rules files had to be smaller than 64 KB. (To be more specific, the compiled AST of the rules file had to be smaller than 64 KB, and you wouldn't know you were within the limit until you tried to deploy the rules.) This limit was holding some developers back, and once you reached the limit, you had to start making tradeoffs in your rules. We definitely wanted to fix this. </p><p>Since this is one of the limits that helps rules return a decision in nanoseconds, we wanted to find a way to increase the limit without sacrificing performance. We optimized how we compile and store the Rules file, and we were able to quadruple the limit to 256 KB!  </p><p>The limits on rules are in place to keep rules fast enough to return a decision in nanoseconds, but we work hard to keep them workable. Let us know if you start to outgrow any of them </p><p>All of these features are informed by the feedback we hear from you about what's great, what's hard, and what's confusing about Firestore Security Rules, so keep letting us know what you think! </p><img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/FirebaseBlog/~4/IRFyi2Uxwx0\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" data-original-width=\"600\" data-original-height=\"600\" />\" />  </div><figcaption>    <strong><div>Shobhit Chugh</div></strong>      <em>Product Manager</em>  </figcaption></figure> <p>Stability is critical to your app’s success, so we’ve continued to enhance Firebase with tools to help you maintain high quality apps. This includes bringing the best of Fabric’s crash reporting tools to Firebase. Many of you have made the move from Fabric to Firebase yet still have questions like “Why do I still have ‘Fabric’ everywhere in my code?” or “Why does it feel like I am in Firebase, yet still in Fabric?” </p><p>We can now lay your questions to rest. We're excited to announce Firebase Crashlytics SDK is in Beta! </p><p>With the Firebase Crashlytics SDK, you can remove all Fabric dependencies, such as references to Fabric’s APIs in your code, giving you a much cleaner codebase.  </p><p>The Firebase Crashlytics SDK is also designed to be intuitive and consistent with all other Firebase SDKs. For example, the Crashlytics SDK has consistent package names, API design, and initialization as other Firebase SDKs. </p><p>The Crashlytics SDK also supports Mac Catalyst apps, in addition to iOS, macOS, and tvOS apps. This new feature makes it easier for you to continue building apps for Apple devices.  </p><p>We’ve also open-sourced the Crashlytics SDK to further increase the extensibility and transparency of our platform.  </p><p><a href=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-091SGoLQodg/XkyKQy7ZFcI/AAAAAAAAEqI/9r10t9-eI4kOHYk_YG_ZfAyCxx2JHW1DgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/crash1.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://3.bp.blogspot.com/-091SGoLQodg/XkyKQy7ZFcI/AAAAAAAAEqI/9r10t9-eI4kOHYk_YG_ZfAyCxx2JHW1DgCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/crash1.png\" data-original-width=\"1494\" data-original-height=\"674\" /></a></p><p><a href=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-SBBBSvkJYZo/XkyKUSqEbYI/AAAAAAAAEqU/ZgXBsALLizEOXiVPZG4GLu-55xixTBmVQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/crash.png\" imageanchor=\"1\" ><img border=\"0\" src=\"https://2.bp.blogspot.com/-SBBBSvkJYZo/XkyKUSqEbYI/AAAAAAAAEqU/ZgXBsALLizEOXiVPZG4GLu-55xixTBmVQCLcBGAsYHQ/s1600/crash.png\" data-original-width=\"1520\" data-original-height=\"814\" /></a></p><p>Combining analytics data and crashes helps you unlock additional insights into your app’s stability. The Firebase Crashlytics SDK works seamlessly with the Google Analytics SDK to provide features such as crash-free statistics, the Latest Release report, and Breadcrumbs. With Breadcrumbs you can see the steps a user took that led to a crash. This insight helps you reproduce and fix issues quickly. Google Analytics replaces Fabric's legacy analytics engine, Answers, so we encourage you to add Google Analytics to your apps. </p><p>Try it out! </p><p>Ready to begin developing with Firebase Crashlytics SDK? If you are upgrading from the legacy Fabric Crashlytics SDK, read our <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/upgrade-sdk\">upgrade guide</a>. Developers new to Crashlytics should begin with our <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/docs/crashlytics/get-started\">getting started docs</a>. Interested in the Crashlytics source code? View our <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/firebase-android-sdk/tree/master/firebase-crashlytics\">Android</a> and <a href=\"https://github.com/firebase/firebase-ios-sdk/tree/master/Crashlytics\">iOS</a> repositories on GitHub. </p><p>We'd love to hear your feedback. As always, you can reach out to us through our <a href=\"https://firebase.google.com/support\">official support page</a>.
